There appear to be a lot of 'NA' throughout this data. That isn't good, as it means that certain samples were not adequate. Certain means are far different than max values as well, such as for Zn_ppm among others. 

Now, it's time to determine the relationship between SOM_percent and Ca_ppm. As these are both forms of continuous data, a scatterplot should do the trick. 


<!-- rnb-text-end -->


<!-- rnb-chunk-begin -->


<!-- rnb-source-begin eyJkYXRhIjoiYGBgclxuYGBgclxuZ2dwbG90KHNvaWwsIGFlcyh4ID0gU09NX3BlcmNlbnQsIHkgPSBDYV9wcG0pKSArXG4gIGdlb21fcG9pbnQoKSArXG4gIHRoZW1lX2J3KClcbmBgYFxuYGBgIn0= -->

<!-- rnb-text-begin -->


Now, I'll need top guess the relationship between variables. Judging from this scatterplot, there is a weak positive relationship between SOM_percent ans Ca_ppm. It appears that there's a general trend, but it isn't too clearly defined and points aren't very close. 

Since both the response and predictor are continuous, linear regression should be used to further analyze this data. 


<!-- rnb-text-end -->


<!-- rnb-chunk-begin -->


<!-- rnb-source-begin eyJkYXRhIjoiYGBgclxuYGBgclxuU09NX0NBX21vZCA8LSBsbShDYV9wcG0gfiBTT01fcGVyY2VudCwgZGF0YSA9IHNvaWwpXG5gYGBcbmBgYCJ9 -->

```r
```r
SOM_CA_mod <- lm(Ca_ppm ~ SOM_percent, data = soil)

<!-- rnb-source-end -->

<!-- rnb-chunk-end -->


<!-- rnb-text-begin -->


Checking model assumptions prior to interpretation.

As p is greater than .05, there is no significant correlation between SOM_percent and Ca_ppm. This demonstrates a null hypothesis--no statistical relationship between the chosen variables.

As there is a difference between the two group means, a two sample t-test should be used. 

  WHY? YOU HAVE COUNT DATA, WHICH IS DISCRETE, BETWEEN TWO SITES. CHI-SQUARE TEST WOULD BE APPROPRIATE. SEE KEY.


<!-- rnb-text-end -->

```r
```r

t.test(Worms ~ Forest, data = Donner_Kip_Worms)

Welch Two Sample t-test

data: Worms by Forest t = -5.078, df = 13.517, p-value = 0.0001877 alternative hypothesis: true difference in means between group DONNERVILLE and group KIP TRACT is not equal to 0 95 percent confidence interval: -3.005781 -1.216441 sample estimates: mean in group DONNERVILLE mean in group KIP TRACT 1.777778 3.888889

From this test, we can tell that there is a statistically significant difference between worms found at Donnerville and those collected from Kip Tract. 

Now, I'll check on the difference between Mg_ppm in soil from Peavine and South Hammond. This code will look similar to the last problem. 


<!-- rnb-text-end -->


<!-- rnb-chunk-begin -->


<!-- rnb-source-begin eyJkYXRhIjoiYGBgclxuUGVhdl9Tb3V0aF9NZyA8LSBzb2lsW2MoMSwgNyldXG5cblBlYXZfU291dGhfTWcgPC0gUGVhdl9Tb3V0aF9NZ1syODo0NSxdXG5gYGAifQ== -->

```r
Peav_South_Mg <- soil[c(1, 7)]

Peav_South_Mg <- Peav_South_Mg[28:45,]

YOU ARE GETTING GOOD AT INDEXING! BUT THINK ABOUT THE FACT THAT YOU DONT NEED TO SPECIFY NEW DATA FRAMES - YOU CAN JUST CALL ON THE VARIABLES YOU NEED FROM THE MAIN SOIL DATAFRAME.

Histogram to check for outliers. It appears that there may be one at approximately 120 ppm, which could throw off data to some degree.

ggplot(Peav_South_Mg, aes(x = Mg_ppm)) +
  geom_histogram()

Ill use a t test again to determine the relationship between these two means (Peavine and South Hammond)

IN THIS CASE, T-TEST IS APPROPRAITE SINCE YOU HAVE A CONTINUOUS RESPONSE AND A CATEGORICAL PREDICTOR

```r
t.test(Mg_ppm ~ Forest, data=Peav_South_Mg)

<!-- rnb-source-end -->

<!-- rnb-output-begin eyJkYXRhIjoiXG5cdFdlbGNoIFR3byBTYW1wbGUgdC10ZXN0XG5cbmRhdGE6ICBNZ19wcG0gYnkgRm9yZXN0XG50ID0gLTEuMjc0OSwgZGYgPSAxNC42MTQsIHAtdmFsdWUgPSAwLjIyMjJcbmFsdGVybmF0aXZlIGh5cG90aGVzaXM6IHRydWUgZGlmZmVyZW5jZSBpbiBtZWFucyBiZXR3ZWVuIGdyb3VwIFBFQVZJTkUgYW5kIGdyb3VwIFNPVVRIIEhBTU1PTkQgaXMgbm90IGVxdWFsIHRvIDBcbjk1IHBlcmNlbnQgY29uZmlkZW5jZSBpbnRlcnZhbDpcbiAtMzguNjQ4OTk1ICAgOS43NjAxMDZcbnNhbXBsZSBlc3RpbWF0ZXM6XG4gICAgICBtZWFuIGluIGdyb3VwIFBFQVZJTkUgbWVhbiBpbiBncm91cCBTT1VUSCBIQU1NT05EIFxuICAgICAgICAgICAgICAgICAgIDQ5LjAwMDAwICAgICAgICAgICAgICAgICAgICA2My40NDQ0NCBcbiJ9 -->

As this p value is greater than .05, there is no statistically significant relationship between Mg_ppm at either site.

I'll use a chi squared test to determine a relationship between categories (all forest types). 

NOPE. YOU AGAIN HAVE CONTINUOUS RESPONSE, NOT COUNT DATA. SO THIS SHOULD BE A ONE-WAY ANOVA. ALWAYS THINK ABOUT THE TYPE OF DATA YOU HAVE WHEN DECIDING ON THE APPROPRIATE TEST.

```r
Zinc_Mat <- xtabs(Zn_ppm ~ Forest, data = Zinc)

chisq.test(Zinc_Mat)

Chi-squared test for given probabilities

data: Zinc_Mat X-squared = 24.467, df = 4, p-value = 6.439e-05 ```
